### [265] [A Task-Centric Theory for Iterative Self-Improvement with Easy-to-Hard Curricula](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10014)
*Chenruo Liu,Yijun Dong,Yiqiu Shen,Qi Lei*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Finite-sample theory for iterative self-improvement of autoregressive LLMs, modeling each round as maximum-likelihood fine-tuning on reward-filtered data, with explicit data-acceptance feedback leading to sustained improvement but eventual saturation; curriculum (easy-to-hard) can outperform fixed task mixtures under certain initialization, task difficulty, and budget; validated by simulations and graph-reasoning experiments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Address the lack of theoretical guarantees for self-improvement loops in finite-sample regimes and understand data-efficiency, the effect of reward filtering, and how curricula influence progress.

Method: Model each self-improvement round as maximum-likelihood fine-tuning on a reward-filtered distribution derived from the LLM’s outputs; derive finite-sample guarantees on expected reward; analyze the data-collection dynamics revealing a feedback loop where better models accept more data per iteration; study task-centric curricula across difficulty levels and prove conditions under which easy-to-hard curricula outperform fixed mixtures; validate with Monte-Carlo simulations and controlled graph-based reasoning experiments.

Result: Finite-sample guarantees for expected reward are derived; the analysis reveals a data-collection feedback loop that supports sustained improvement and explains saturation; under certain initialization, task difficulty, and budget, easy-to-hard curricula provably beat fixed mixtures; empirical validation via simulations and graph-based tasks corroborates the theory.

Conclusion: The work provides a rigorous finite-sample theoretical foundation for iterative self-improvement in LLMs, clarifies how reward-filtered data and task curricula influence data efficiency and long-run gains, and demonstrates practical relevance through simulations and controlled experiments.

Abstract: Iterative self-improvement fine-tunes an autoregressive large language model (LLM) on reward-verified outputs generated by the LLM itself. In contrast to the empirical success of self-improvement, the theoretical foundation of this generative, iterative procedure in a practical, finite-sample setting remains limited. We make progress toward this goal by modeling each round of self-improvement as maximum-likelihood fine-tuning on a reward-filtered distribution and deriving finite-sample guarantees for the expected reward. Our analysis reveals an explicit feedback loop where better models accept more data per iteration, supporting sustained self-improvement while explaining eventual saturation of such improvement. Adopting a task-centric view by considering reasoning tasks with multiple difficulty levels, we further prove quantifiable conditions on model initialization, task difficulty, and sample budget where easy-to-hard curricula provably achieve better guarantees than training on fixed mixtures of tasks. Our analyses are validated via Monte-Carlo simulations and controlled experiments on graph-based reasoning tasks.

### [266] [Position: Message-passing and spectral GNNs are two sides of the same coin](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10031)
*Antonis Vasileiou,Juan Cervino,Pascal Frossard,Charilaos I. Kanatsoulis,Christopher Morris,Michael T. Schaub,Pierre Vandergheynst,Zhiyang Wang,Guy Wolf,Ron Levie*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Unifies MPNNs and spectral GNNs as permutation-equivariant graph-signal operators; shows similar expressivity in many cases, with gaps only in specific regimes; advocates leveraging complementary strengths and a shared framework to accelerate progress.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The traditional division between message-passing (discrete) and spectral (continuous/signal-processing) GNNs is artificial and impedes theoretical and practical advances. A unified, principled view can guide architecture design and understanding.

Method: Conceptual unification: treat MPNNs and spectral GNNs as distinct parametrizations of permutation-equivariant operators on graph signals. Analyze expressivity, equivalence, and regimes where gaps exist; discuss complementary strengths (discrete-structure reasoning vs spectral properties).

Result: Many popular GNN architectures have equivalent expressive power under the unified view; genuine expressive gaps appear only in limited regimes. The spectral perspective clarifies smoothing, bottlenecks, stability, and community structure, while MPNNs clarify discrete structure and isomorphism-based reasoning.

Conclusion: A common theoretical framework linking MPNNs and spectral GNNs will accelerate progress in graph learning by enabling cross-pollination and unified analysis rather than treating the two paradigms as separate.

Abstract: Graph neural networks (GNNs) are commonly divided into message-passing neural networks (MPNNs) and spectral graph neural networks, reflecting two largely separate research traditions in machine learning and signal processing. This paper argues that this divide is mostly artificial, hindering progress in the field. We propose a viewpoint in which both MPNNs and spectral GNNs are understood as different parametrizations of permutation-equivariant operators acting on graph signals. From this perspective, many popular architectures are equivalent in expressive power, while genuine gaps arise only in specific regimes. We further argue that MPNNs and spectral GNNs offer complementary strengths. That is, MPNNs provide a natural language for discrete structure and expressivity analysis using tools from logic and graph isomorphism research, while the spectral perspective provides principled tools for understanding smoothing, bottlenecks, stability, and community structure. Overall, we posit that progress in graph learning will be accelerated by clearly understanding the key similarities and differences between these two types of GNNs, and by working towards unifying these perspectives within a common theoretical and conceptual framework rather than treating them as competing paradigms.

### [267] [Effectiveness of Binary Autoencoders for QUBO-Based Optimization Problems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10037)
*Tetsuro Abe,Masashi Yamashita,Shu Tanaka*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A binary autoencoder (bAE) for FMQA aligns latent space with solution geometry, improving efficiency in black-box optimization over permutations by preserving feasibility and smoothing neighborhoods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Nonbinary decision spaces (e.g., permutations) suffer from poor search efficiency when encoded into binary variables for FMQA. The goal is to learn latent binary representations that respect the original neighborhood structure and feasibility, improving optimization performance.

Method: Train a bAE on feasible solutions to produce a compact binary latent code. Integrate with FMQA to optimize the surrogate in latent space. Compare against manually designed encodings on a small traveling salesman problem (TSP) as an interpretable testbed. Analyze reconstruction accuracy, alignment of objective-distance with latent Hamming-distance, neighborhood smoothness under bit flips, and prevalence of local optima.

Result: The bAE reconstructs feasible tours accurately; tour distances align better with latent Hamming distances than manual encodings; neighborhoods under small bit flips are smoother; fewer local optima are observed. These geometric properties explain faster convergence in approximation ratio while maintaining feasibility, illustrating how latent representations benefit black-box optimization.

Conclusion: Latent representations learned by bAE explain performance gains in FMQA for permutation-like problems and offer guidance for designing encodings in black-box optimization. The approach supports improved search efficiency and feasibility preservation, with potential applicability to broader nonbinary optimization tasks.

Abstract: In black-box combinatorial optimization, objective evaluations are often expensive, so high quality solutions must be found under a limited budget. Factorization machine with quantum annealing (FMQA) builds a quadratic surrogate model from evaluated samples and optimizes it on an Ising machine. However, FMQA requires binary decision variables, and for nonbinary structures such as integer permutations, the choice of binary encoding strongly affects search efficiency. If the encoding fails to reflect the original neighborhood structure, small Hamming moves may not correspond to meaningful modifications in the original solution space, and constrained problems can yield many infeasible candidates that waste evaluations. Recent work combines FMQA with a binary autoencoder (bAE) that learns a compact binary latent code from feasible solutions, yet the mechanism behind its performance gains is unclear. Using a small traveling salesman problem as an interpretable testbed, we show that the bAE reconstructs feasible tours accurately and, compared with manually designed encodings at similar compression, better aligns tour distances with latent Hamming distances, yields smoother neighborhoods under small bit flips, and produces fewer local optima. These geometric properties explain why bAE+FMQA improves the approximation ratio faster while maintaining feasibility throughout optimization, and they provide guidance for designing latent representations for black-box optimization.

### [268] [Vendi Novelty Scores for Out-of-Distribution Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10062)
*Amey P. Pasarkar,Adji Bousso Dieng*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A diversity-based OOD detector using Vendi Scores (VNS). It detects novelty by measuring how a test sample increases the diversity score of in-distribution features, without density modeling, achieving state-of-the-art results with linear-time, non-parametric computation and even using only 1% of training data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: OOD detection often relies on model confidence or likelihood with restrictive assumptions. This work proposes a third paradigm: novelty is captured through diversity among feature representations, leveraging Vendi Scores to quantify how a test sample increases the diversity of the in-distribution feature set.

Method: Define Vendi Novelty Score (VNS) as the increase in the Vendi Scores (VS) when a test sample is added to the in-distribution feature set. VS is a family of similarity-based diversity metrics. VNS is linear-time, non-parametric, and combines class-conditional (local) and dataset-level (global) novelty signals without density estimation.

Result: Empirically, VNS yields state-of-the-art OOD detection performance across multiple image classification benchmarks and architectures. It remains effective with only 1% of the training data, enabling deployment in memory- or access-constrained settings.

Conclusion: A principled, density-free, diversity-based approach to OOD detection. VNS leverages both local and global novelty signals, is computationally efficient, and delivers strong performance across benchmarks, including under data-scarce training conditions.

Abstract: Out-of-distribution (OOD) detection is critical for the safe deployment of machine learning systems. Existing post-hoc detectors typically rely on model confidence scores or likelihood estimates in feature space, often under restrictive distributional assumptions. In this work, we introduce a third paradigm and formulate OOD detection from a diversity perspective. We propose the Vendi Novelty Score (VNS), an OOD detector based on the Vendi Scores (VS), a family of similarity-based diversity metrics. VNS quantifies how much a test sample increases the VS of the in-distribution feature set, providing a principled notion of novelty that does not require density modeling. VNS is linear-time, non-parametric, and naturally combines class-conditional (local) and dataset-level (global) novelty signals. Across multiple image classification benchmarks and network architectures, VNS achieves state-of-the-art OOD detection performance. Remarkably, VNS retains this performance when computed using only 1% of the training data, enabling deployment in memory- or access-constrained settings.

</details>


### [269] [Learning on the Manifold: Unlocking Standard Diffusion Transformers with Representation Encoders](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10099)
*Amandeep Kumar,Vishal M. Patel*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: RJF enables diffusion transformers on representation encoders to converge without width scaling by aligning diffusion trajectories with the representation manifold's geodesics and correcting curvature-induced errors, achieving competitive FID (3.37) on DiT-B (131M) where prior methods failed.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard diffusion transformers fail to converge when operating in latent representation spaces due to geometric misalignment: probability paths are forced through low-density interiors rather than along the manifold surface. This geometric interference, not just capacity bottlenecks, limits performance.

Method: Propose Riemannian Flow Matching with Jacobi Regularization (RJF). Constrain the generative process to follow manifold geodesics and mitigate curvature-induced error propagation via Jacobi-based regularization. Validate on the DiT-B (131M) architecture without width scaling.

Result: The DiT-B architecture converges with RJF and achieves an FID of 3.37, whereas prior methods fail to converge. Code is released at the provided URL.

Conclusion: A geometric, manifold-aware formulation of diffusion for representation encoders resolves convergence issues without width scaling, expanding the applicability of diffusion transformers to latent spaces and offering a scalable path for high-fidelity generative modeling.

Abstract: Leveraging representation encoders for generative modeling offers a path for efficient, high-fidelity synthesis. However, standard diffusion transformers fail to converge on these representations directly. While recent work attributes this to a capacity bottleneck proposing computationally expensive width scaling of diffusion transformers we demonstrate that the failure is fundamentally geometric. We identify Geometric Interference as the root cause: standard Euclidean flow matching forces probability paths through the low-density interior of the hyperspherical feature space of representation encoders, rather than following the manifold surface. To resolve this, we propose Riemannian Flow Matching with Jacobi Regularization (RJF). By constraining the generative process to the manifold geodesics and correcting for curvature-induced error propagation, RJF enables standard Diffusion Transformer architectures to converge without width scaling. Our method RJF enables the standard DiT-B architecture (131M parameters) to converge effectively, achieving an FID of 3.37 where prior methods fail to converge. Code: https://github.com/amandpkr/RJF

</details>


### [270] [WildCat: Near-Linear Attention in Theory and Practice](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10056)
*Tobias Schröder,Lester Mackey*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: WildCat compresses attention via a small weighted coreset chosen by pivoted Cholesky, achieving near-linear-time attention with super-polynomial error decay and strong fidelity guarantees, validated on vision and language tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Attention scales quadratically with sequence length, making deployment costly; there is a need for fast, memory-efficient attention with theoretical guarantees of fidelity.

Method: Construct a small coreset of attention inputs using spectrally accurate subsampling via random pivoted Cholesky; compute optimal weights to minimize reconstruction error; achieve near-linear runtime O(n^{1+o(1)}) and provide bounded-input error guarantees (O(n^{-√log log n})); implement with GPU-accelerated PyTorch and evaluate on image generation, image classification, and language model KV-cache tasks.

Result: WildCat attains high fidelity to exact attention with super-polynomial error decay under bounded inputs and scales near-linearly in sequence length; demonstrates practical benefits over prior approximations in vision and language benchmarks.

Conclusion: WildCat offers a provably accurate, scalable attention compression method with strong practical performance, enabling efficient attention in large-scale models.

Abstract: We introduce WildCat, a high-accuracy, low-cost approach to compressing the attention mechanism in neural networks. While attention is a staple of modern network architectures, it is also notoriously expensive to deploy due to resource requirements that scale quadratically with the input sequence length $n$. WildCat avoids these quadratic costs by only attending over a small weighted coreset. Crucially, we select the coreset using a fast but spectrally-accurate subsampling algorithm -- randomly pivoted Cholesky -- and weight the elements optimally to minimise reconstruction error. Remarkably, given bounded inputs, WildCat approximates exact attention with super-polynomial $O(n^{-\sqrt{\log(\log(n))}})$ error decay while running in near-linear $O(n^{1+o(1)})$ time. In contrast, prior practical approximations either lack error guarantees or require quadratic runtime to guarantee such high fidelity. We couple this advance with a GPU-optimized PyTorch implementation and a suite of benchmark experiments demonstrating the benefits of WildCat for image generation, image classification, and language model KV cache compression.

</details>


### [271] [Features as Rewards: Scalable Supervision for Open-Ended Tasks via Interpretability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10067)
*Aaditya Vikram Prasad,Connor Watts,Jack Merullo,Dhruvil Gala,Owen Lewis,Thomas McGrath,Ekdeep Singh Lubana*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A novel RL framework (RLFR) uses model features as reward signals to reduce hallucinations in open-ended generation, grounded in a hallucination-probing framework. It intervenes to correct uncertain outputs and enables scalable test-time supervision. On Gemma-3-12B-IT, it yields a policy 58% less prone to hallucinations while preserving standard benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mitigate hallucination in large language models by leveraging interpretable feature-based supervision as scalable rewards, enabling open-ended tasks to benefit from stabilizing feedback without brute-force supervision.

Method: Develop RLFR: train a policy with feature-based rewards; use a hallucination-probing framework to identify candidate hallucinations; the agent learns to intervene and correct completions when factuality is uncertain; apply end-to-end on Gemma-3-12B-IT to obtain a less-hallucinatory model with maintained performance; allow scalable test-time compute guided by feature rewards.

Result: Policy achieves 58% reduction in hallucinations compared to the original model while preserving performance on standard benchmarks.

Conclusion: Grounding supervision in interpretable feature language enables a new paradigm for learning open-ended tasks, combining interpretability with scalable RL-based control to reduce hallucinations.

Abstract: Language models trained on large-scale datasets have been shown to learn features that encode abstract concepts such as factuality or intent. Such features are traditionally used for test-time monitoring or steering. We present an alternative affordance: features as scalable supervision for open-ended tasks. We consider the case of hallucination-reduction as a desirable, yet open-ended behavior and design a reinforcement learning (RL) pipeline, titled RLFR (Reinforcement Learning from Feature Rewards), that uses features as reward functions. Grounded in a novel probing framework that identifies candidate hallucinated claims, our pipeline teaches a model to intervene and correct its completions when it is uncertain of their factuality. Furthermore, the pipeline enables scalable test-time compute, guided once more by our reward features. This end-to-end process operationalized on Gemma-3-12B-IT results in a policy that is 58% less likely to hallucinate compared to the original model, while preserving performance on standard benchmarks. Taken together, by grounding supervision in the language of features, this paper introduces a novel paradigm in the use of interpretability for learning open-ended tasks.

</details>


### [272] [Towards Explainable Federated Learning: Understanding the Impact of Differential Privacy](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.10100)
*Júlio Oliveira,Rodrigo Ferreira,André Riker,Glaucio H. S. Carvalho,Eirini Eleni Tsilopoulou*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Introduces Federated EXplainable Trees with Differential Privacy (FEXT-DP): a tree-based federated learning model that adds differential privacy to enhance data privacy while maintaining explainability. Reported benefits include faster training (fewer rounds), lower mean squared error (MSE), and improved explainability, with DP having a potential negative impact on interpretability, indicating a privacy-utility-interpretability trade-off.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To reconcile data privacy and explainability in ML by combining Federated Learning with Differential Privacy in a tree-based, interpretable model, addressing the trade-off between privacy protection and model transparency.

Method: Develops FEXT-DP, a decision-tree–based FL framework that integrates differential privacy into the tree-building or aggregation process to protect data while preserving interpretability. The approach emphasizes lightweight, explainable models (trees) over neural nets and evaluates training efficiency and explainability.

Result: Empirical evaluation reports improved training efficiency (fewer FL rounds), reduced MSE, and improved explainability for FEXT-DP, with a noted side-effect that DP can degrade explainability.

Conclusion: FEXT-DP demonstrates that it is feasible to combine privacy-preserving DP with explainable, tree-based FL to achieve faster training and better accuracy while maintaining interpretable models, though care must be taken to mitigate DP-induced reductions in explainability.

Abstract: Data privacy and eXplainable Artificial Intelligence (XAI) are two important aspects for modern Machine Learning systems. To enhance data privacy, recent machine learning models have been designed as a Federated Learning (FL) system. On top of that, additional privacy layers can be added, via Differential Privacy (DP). On the other hand, to improve explainability, ML must consider more interpretable approaches with reduced number of features and less complex internal architecture. In this context, this paper aims to achieve a machine learning (ML) model that combines enhanced data privacy with explainability. So, we propose a FL solution, called Federated EXplainable Trees with Differential Privacy (FEXT-DP), that: (i) is based on Decision Trees, since they are lightweight and have superior explainability than neural networks-based FL systems; (ii) provides additional layer of data privacy protection applying Differential Privacy (DP) to the Tree-Based model. However, there is a side effect adding DP: it harms the explainability of the system. So, this paper also presents the impact of DP protection on the explainability of the ML model. The carried out performance assessment shows improvements of FEXT-DP in terms of a faster training, i.e., numbers of rounds, Mean Squared Error and explainability.

</details>

### [273] [Fréchet Distance in the Imbalanced Case](https://arxiv.org/abs/2602.09551)
*Lotte Blank*

Main category: cs.CG

TL;DR: The abstract presents tight conditional lower bounds for approximating the Fréchet distance across 1D, 2D, and higher dimensions under OVH, and matching/near-matching approximation algorithms that nearly close the gap between lower and upper bounds across continuous/discrete Fréchet distances in Euclidean and L∞ spaces, including a general result for any Lp space.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Understand the computational complexity of approximating Fréchet distance between polygonal curves and establish conditional lower bounds, while providing near-optimal algorithms to narrow the gap between worst-case hardness and algorithmic performance.

Method: Reductions from the Orthogonal Vectors Hypothesis (OVH) to construct hard instances for 1D and higher-dimensional Fréchet distance problems. Extend constructions to 2D with continuous/discrete variants; analyze implications for Euclidean and L∞ metrics; develop approximation algorithms with provable guarantees and analyze time complexities for both 1D and general Lp spaces.

Result: - In 1D discrete Fréchet distance, cannot approximate within factor 2−ε in O((nm)^{1−δ}) time for any ε,δ>0 unless OVH fails. - In 2D, lower bound extends to 1+√2−ε (Euclidean) or 3−ε (L∞) under similar assumptions; strengthens previous bounds to the m=n^α regime with α∈(0,1); improves Bringmann’s 1.001 factor. - For 1D discrete Fréchet, an approximation algorithm achieving optimal approximation factor with near-optimal running time. - For curves in any dimension under any Lp space, a (3+ε)-approximation algorithm for continuous/discrete Fréchet with time O((n+m^2) log n), nearly matching the lower bound in L∞ case.

Conclusion: The work sharpens conditional hardness results for Fréchet distance approximations and delivers near-optimal algorithms, showing robustness of lower bounds across dimensions and metrics, and providing a broad, practical approximation framework for continuous and discrete Fréchet distance in Lp spaces.

Abstract: Given two polygonal curves $P$ and $Q$ defined by $n$ and $m$ vertices with $m\leq n$, we show that the discrete Fréchet distance in 1D cannot be approximated within a factor of $2-\varepsilon$ in $\mathcal{O}((nm)^{1-δ})$ time for any $\varepsilon, δ>0$ unless OVH fails. Using a similar construction, we extend this bound for curves in 2D under the continuous or discrete Fréchet distance and increase the approximation factor to $1+\sqrt{2}-\varepsilon$ (resp. $3-\varepsilon$) if the curves lie in the Euclidean space (resp. in the $L_\infty$-space). This strengthens the lower bound by Buchin, Ophelders, and Speckmann to the case where $m=n^α$ for $α\in(0,1)$ and increases the approximation factor of $1.001$ by Bringmann. For the discrete Fréchet distance in 1D, we provide an approximation algorithm with optimal approximation factor and almost optimal running time. Further, for curves in any dimension embedded in any $L_p$ space, we present a $(3+\varepsilon)$-approximation algorithm for the continuous and discrete Fréchet distance using $\mathcal{O}((n+m^2)\log n)$ time, which almost matches the approximation factor of the lower bound for the $L_\infty$ metric.

</details>
